<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 74 languages"   >

	</label>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"DELL" and "Dell Inc." redirect here. For its parent company, see <a href="/wiki/Dell_Technologies" title="Dell Technologies">Dell Technologies</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dell_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dell (disambiguation)">Dell (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Dell Inc.</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Technology_company" title="Technology company">technology company</a> that develops, sells, repairs, and supports <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computers</a> and related products and services. Dell is owned by its parent company, <a href="/wiki/Dell_Technologies" title="Dell Technologies">Dell Technologies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-statesman_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statesman-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell sells <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computers</a> (PCs), <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">servers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Data_storage_device" class="mw-redirect" title="Data storage device">data storage devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Network_switch" title="Network switch">network switches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_software" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer software">software</a>, computer <a href="/wiki/Peripheral" title="Peripheral">peripherals</a>, <a href="/wiki/HDTV" class="mw-redirect" title="HDTV">HDTVs</a>, cameras, printers, and electronics built by other manufacturers. The company is known for how it manages its <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain_management" title="Supply chain management">supply chain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic commerce">electronic commerce</a>. This includes Dell selling directly to customers and delivering PCs that the customer wants.<sup id="cite_ref-bw1103_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw1103-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-statesman_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statesman-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Dell was a pure hardware vendor until 2009 when it acquired <a href="/wiki/Perot_Systems" title="Perot Systems">Perot Systems</a>. Dell then entered the market for IT services. The company has expanded storage and networking systems. In the late 2000s, it began expanding from offering computers only to delivering a range of technology for enterprise customers.<sup id="cite_ref-reut_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reut-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell is a subsidiary of Dell Technologies, Inc., a publicly <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">traded company</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nasdaq" title="Nasdaq">Nasdaq</a>:&#160;DELL), as well as a component of the <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ-100" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ-100">NASDAQ-100</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a>. Dell is ranked 31st on the Fortune 500 list in 2022,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> up from 76th in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It is also the sixth-largest company in Texas by total revenue, according to <i>Fortune</i> magazine. It is the second-largest non-oil company in Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> As of 2024,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> it is the <a href="/wiki/Market_share_of_personal_computer_vendors" title="Market share of personal computer vendors">world's third-largest personal computer vendor</a> by unit sales, after <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a> and <a href="/wiki/HP_Inc." title="HP Inc.">HP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, Dell acquired the enterprise technology firm <a href="/wiki/EMC_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="EMC Corporation">EMC Corporation</a>. Dell and EMC became divisions of Dell Technologies. Dell EMC sells data storage, information security, <a href="/wiki/Virtualization" title="Virtualization">virtualization</a>, analytics, and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Dell_logo.svg/300px-Dell_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="127" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Dell's former logo, used from 1992 to 2018</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Dell_Logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Dell&#39;s logo, used before the acquisition of EMC, used from 2010 to 2018" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Dell_Logo.svg/150px-Dell_Logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="150" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Dell_Logo.svg/225px-Dell_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Dell_Logo.svg/300px-Dell_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Dell's logo used before the acquisition of EMC, used from 2010 to 2018</div></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_start-up">Founding and start-up</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding and start-up"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Dell" title="Michael Dell">Michael Dell</a> founded Dell Computer Corporation, doing business as <i>PC's Limited</i> in 1984 while a student at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> operating from Michael Dell's off-campus dormitory room at <a href="/wiki/Dobie_Center" title="Dobie Center">Dobie Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The start-up aimed to sell <a href="/wiki/IBM_PC_compatible" title="IBM PC compatible">IBM PC compatible</a> computers built from stock components. Michael Dell started trading in the belief that, by selling personal computer systems directly to customers, PC's Limited could better understand customers' needs and provide the most effective computing solutions to meet those needs.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Dell dropped out of college upon completion of his freshman year at the University of Texas in order to focus full-time on his fledgling business, after getting about $1,000 in expansion-capital from his family.<sup id="cite_ref-delltimeline_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delltimeline-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As of April 2021, Dell's net worth was estimated to be over $50 billion (equivalent to $55,470,000,000&#32;in 2023).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg/220px-PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg/330px-PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg/440px-PC%27s_Limited_Turbo_PC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4464" data-file-height="3352" /></a><figcaption>PC's Limited Turbo PC, circa 1985</figcaption></figure><p>In 1985, the company produced the first computer of its own design, the "Turbo PC", selling for US$795 (equivalent to $1,913&#32;in 2023)<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and containing an <a href="/wiki/Intel_8088" title="Intel 8088">Intel 8088</a>-compatible processor capable of running at a maximum speed of 8&#160;MHz.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> PC's Limited advertised the systems in national computer magazines for sale directly to consumers, and custom assembled each ordered unit according to a selection of options. This offered buyers prices lower than those of retail brands, but with greater convenience than assembling the components themselves. Pc's Limited was not the first company to use this business model, but they became one of the first to succeed with it. The company grossed more than $73 million in its first year of trading.
</p><p>The company dropped the <i>PC's Limited</i> name in 1987 to become Dell Computer Corporation and began expanding globally. The reasoning was that this new company name better reflected its presence in the business market, and also resolved issues with the use of "Limited" in a company name in certain countries.<sup id="cite_ref-ferrell198708_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ferrell198708-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The company set up its first international operations in Britain; 11 more followed within the next four years. In June 1988, Dell Computer's market capitalization grew by $30 million to $80 million (equivalent to $177,850,000&#32;in 2023) from its June 22 initial public offering of 3.5 million shares at $8.50 a share.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, Dell Computer set up its first on-site service programs in order to compensate for the lack of local retailers prepared to act as service centers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_in_the_1990s_and_early_2000s">Growth in the 1990s and early 2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Growth in the 1990s and early 2000s"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg/220px-Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg/330px-Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg/440px-Dell_Latitude_CPx.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a><figcaption>Dell Latitude CPx laptop</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1990, Dell Computer tried selling its products indirectly through warehouse clubs and computer superstores, but met with little success, and the company re-focused on its more successful direct-to-consumer sales model. In 1992, <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> included Dell Computer Corporation in its list of the world's <a href="/wiki/Fortune_Global_500" title="Fortune Global 500">500</a> largest companies, making Michael Dell the youngest CEO of a Fortune 500 company at that time.
</p><p>In 1993, to complement its own direct sales channel, Dell planned to sell PCs at big-box retail outlets such as <a href="/wiki/Wal-Mart" class="mw-redirect" title="Wal-Mart">Wal-Mart</a>, which would have brought in an additional $125 million (equivalent to $238,100,000&#32;in 2023) in annual revenue. <a href="/wiki/Bain_Capital" title="Bain Capital">Bain</a> consultant <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Rollins" title="Kevin Rollins">Kevin Rollins</a> persuaded Michael Dell to pull out of these deals, believing they would be money losers in the long run.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Margins at retail were thin at best and Dell left the reseller channel in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-mhhe.com_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhhe.com-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Rollins would soon join Dell full-time and eventually become the company president and CEO.
</p><p>Originally, Dell did not emphasize the consumer market, due to the higher costs and low profit margins in selling to individuals and households; this changed when the company's Internet site took off in 1996 and 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-delltimeline_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delltimeline-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> While the industry's average selling price to individuals was going down, Dell's was going up, as second- and third-time computer buyers who wanted powerful computers with multiple features and did not need much technical support were choosing Dell. Dell found an opportunity among PC-savvy individuals who liked the convenience of buying direct, customizing their PC to their means, and having it delivered in days. In early 1997, Dell created an internal sales and marketing group dedicated to serving the home market and introduced a product line designed especially for individual users.<sup id="cite_ref-mhhe.com_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhhe.com-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1997 to 2004, Dell steadily grew and it gained market share from competitors even during industry slumps. During the same period, rival PC vendors such as <a href="/wiki/Compaq" title="Compaq">Compaq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gateway,_Inc." title="Gateway, Inc.">Gateway</a>, <a href="/wiki/IBM_Aptiva" title="IBM Aptiva">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Packard_Bell" title="Packard Bell">Packard Bell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AST_Research" title="AST Research">AST Research</a> struggled and eventually left the market or were bought out.<sup id="cite_ref-ZDA_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZDA-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Dell surpassed Compaq to become the largest PC manufacturer in 1999. Operating costs made up only 10 percent of Dell's $35 billion in revenue in 2002 (equivalent to $56,680,000,000&#32;in 2023), compared with 21 percent of revenue at Hewlett-Packard, 25 percent at Gateway, and 46 percent at Cisco.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, when Compaq merged with Hewlett-Packard (the fourth-place PC maker), the newly combined Hewlett-Packard took the top spot for a time but struggled and Dell soon regained its lead. Dell grew the fastest in the early 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-bw1103_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw1103-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2002, Dell expanded its product line to include televisions, <a href="/wiki/Handhelds" class="mw-redirect" title="Handhelds">handhelds</a>, digital audio players, and <a href="/wiki/Printer_(computing)" title="Printer (computing)">printers</a>. Chairman and CEO Michael Dell had repeatedly blocked President and COO <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Rollins" title="Kevin Rollins">Kevin Rollins</a>'s attempt to lessen the company's heavy dependency on PCs, which Rollins wanted to fix by acquiring <a href="/wiki/EMC_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="EMC Corporation">EMC Corporation</a>; a move that would eventually occur over 12 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNMoney-dilemma_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNMoney-dilemma-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, at the annual company meeting, the stockholders approved changing the company name to "Dell Inc." to recognize the company's expansion beyond computers.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-Inc-May-2003-PRE-14A_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-Inc-May-2003-PRE-14A-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, the company announced that it would build a new assembly-plant near <a href="/wiki/Winston-Salem,_North_Carolina" title="Winston-Salem, North Carolina">Winston-Salem</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>; the city and county provided Dell with $37.2 million in incentive packages; the state provided approximately $250 million (equivalent to $386,600,000&#32;in 2023) in incentives and tax breaks. In July, Michael Dell stepped aside as <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a> while retaining his position as <a href="/wiki/Chair_(official)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chair (official)">chairman of the board</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-Inc-May-2004-DEF-14A_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-Inc-May-2004-DEF-14A-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Rollins" title="Kevin Rollins">Kevin Rollins</a>, who had held a number of executive posts at Dell, became the new CEO. Despite no longer holding the CEO title, Dell essentially acted as a de facto co-CEO with Rollins.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNMoney-dilemma_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNMoney-dilemma-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Rollins, Dell purchased the computer hardware manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Alienware" title="Alienware">Alienware</a> in 2006. Dell Inc.'s plan anticipated Alienware continuing to operate independently under its existing management. Alienware expected to benefit from Dell's efficient manufacturing system.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_events">Key events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Key events"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg/220px-DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg/330px-DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg/440px-DELL_AIXM_X51v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648" /></a><figcaption>Dell Axim X51v</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 2005, while earnings and sales continued to rise, sales growth slowed considerably, and the company stock lost 25% of its value that year.<sup id="cite_ref-BW0206_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW0206-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> By June 2006, the stock traded around US$25 which was 40% down from July 2005—the high-water mark of the company in the post-dotcom era.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes2006_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes2006-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> By June 2021, the stock had reached an all-time high of over US$100 per share, reflecting the company's successful transition to a technology solutions provider that helps customers navigate digital transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-Yahoo_Finance_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo_Finance-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The slowing sales growth has been attributed to the maturing PC market, which constituted 66% of Dell's sales, and analysts suggested that Dell needed to make inroads into non-PC business segments such as storage, services, and servers. Dell's price advantage was tied to its ultra-lean manufacturing for desktop PCs,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> but this became less important as savings became harder to find inside the company's supply chain, and as competitors such as Hewlett-Packard and <a href="/wiki/Acer_Inc." title="Acer Inc.">Acer</a> made their PC manufacturing operations more efficient to match Dell, weakening Dell's traditional price differentiation.<sup id="cite_ref-news.cnet.com_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.cnet.com-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the entire PC industry, declines in prices along with commensurate increases in performance meant that Dell had fewer opportunities to upsell to their customers. As a result, the company was selling a greater proportion of inexpensive PCs than before, which eroded profit margins.<sup id="cite_ref-ZDA_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZDA-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The laptop segment had become the fastest-growing of the PC market, but Dell produced low-cost notebooks in China like other PC manufacturers which eliminated Dell's manufacturing cost advantages, plus Dell's reliance on Internet sales meant that it missed out on growing notebook sales in big box stores.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes2006_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes2006-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <i>CNET</i> has suggested that Dell was getting trapped in the increasing commoditization of high volume low margin computers, which prevented it from offering more exciting devices that consumers demanded.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite plans of expanding into other global regions and product segments, Dell was heavily dependent on US corporate PC market, as desktop PCs sold to both commercial and corporate customers accounted for 32 percent of its revenue, 85 percent of its revenue comes from businesses, and 64 percent of its revenue comes from North and South America, according to its 2006 third-quarter results. US shipments of desktop PCs were shrinking, and the corporate PC market, which purchases PCs in upgrade cycles, had largely decided to take a break from buying new systems. The last cycle started around 2002, three or so years after companies started buying PCs ahead of the perceived <a href="/wiki/Year_2000_problem" title="Year 2000 problem">Y2K</a> problems, and corporate clients were not expected to upgrade again until extensive testing of Microsoft's <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a> (expected in early 2007), putting the next upgrade cycle around 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Heavily dependent on PCs, Dell had to slash prices to boost sales volumes, while demanding deep cuts from suppliers.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNMoney-dilemma_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNMoney-dilemma-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell had long stuck by its direct sales model. Consumers had become the main drivers of PC sales in recent years,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> yet there had a decline in consumers purchasing PCs through the Web or on the phone, as increasing numbers were visiting consumer electronics retail stores to try out the devices first. Dell's rivals in the PC industry, HP, Gateway and <a href="/wiki/Acer_Inc." title="Acer Inc.">Acer</a>, had a long retail presence and so were well poised to take advantage of the consumer shift.<sup id="cite_ref-director1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-director1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The lack of a retail presence stymied Dell's attempts to offer consumer electronics such as flat-panel TVs and MP3 players.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Dell responded by experimenting with mall kiosks, plus quasi-retail stores in Texas and New York.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell had a reputation as a company that relied upon supply chain efficiencies to sell established technologies at low prices, instead of being an innovator.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNMoney-dilemma_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNMoney-dilemma-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-director1_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-director1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By the mid-2000s many analysts were looking to innovating companies as the next source of growth in the technology sector. Dell's low spending on R&amp;D relative to its revenue (compared to <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>)&#8212;which worked well in the commoditized PC market—prevented it from making inroads into more lucrative segments, such as MP3 players and later mobile devices.<sup id="cite_ref-BW0206_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW0206-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Increasing spending on R&amp;D would have cut into the operating margins that the company emphasized.<sup id="cite_ref-bw1103_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw1103-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Dell had done well with a horizontal organization that focused on PCs when the computing industry moved to horizontal mix-and-match layers in the 1980s, but by the mid-2000 the industry shifted to vertically integrated stacks to deliver an end-to-end IT product, and Dell lagged far behind competitors like Hewlett-Packard and Oracle.<sup id="cite_ref-news.cnet.com_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.cnet.com-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell's reputation for poor customer service, which was exacerbated as it moved call centers offshore and as its growth outstripped its technical support infrastructure, came under increasing scrutiny on the Web. The original Dell model was known for high customer satisfaction when PCs sold for thousands of dollars but by the 2000s, the company could not justify that level of service when computers in the same line-up sold for hundreds of dollars.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Rollins responded by shifting Dick Hunter from the head of manufacturing to head of customer service. Hunter, who noted that Dell's DNA of cost-cutting "got in the way," aimed to reduce call transfer times and have call center representatives resolve inquiries in one call. By 2006, Dell had spent $100 million in just a few months to improve on this and rolled out <i>DellConnect</i> to answer customer inquiries more quickly. In July 2006, the company started its Direct2Dell blog, and then in February 2007, Michael Dell launched IdeaStorm.com, asking customers for advice including selling Linux computers and reducing the promotional "bloatware" on PCs. These initiatives did manage to cut the negative blog posts from 49% to 22%, as well as reduce the "Dell Hell" prominent on Internet search engines.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes2006_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes2006-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There was also criticism that Dell used faulty components for its PCs, particularly the 11.8 million OptiPlex desktop computers sold to businesses and governments from May 2003 to July 2005 that suffered from <a href="/wiki/Capacitor_plague" title="Capacitor plague">faulty capacitors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> A battery recall in August 2006, as a result of a Dell laptop catching fire, caused much negative attention for the company though later, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> was found responsible for the manufacturing of the batteries, however a Sony spokesman said the problem concerned the combination of the battery with a charger, which was specific to Dell.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>2006 marked the first year that Dell's growth was slower than the PC industry as a whole. By the fourth quarter of 2006, Dell lost its title of the largest PC manufacturer to Hewlett Packard whose Personal Systems Group was invigorated thanks to a restructuring initiated by their CEO <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hurd" title="Mark Hurd">Mark Hurd</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BW0206_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BW0206-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SEC_investigation">SEC investigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: SEC investigation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In August 2005, Dell became the subject of an informal investigation by the United States <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, the company disclosed that the US Attorney for the Southern District of New York had subpoenaed documents related to the company's financial reporting dating back to 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The company delayed filing financial reports for the third and fourth fiscal quarter of 2006, and several class-action lawsuits were filed.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Dell Inc's failure to file its quarterly earnings report could have subjected the company to de-listing from the <a href="/wiki/Nasdaq" title="Nasdaq">Nasdaq</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> but the exchange granted Dell a waiver, allowing the stock to trade normally.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In August 2007, the Company announced that it would restate its earnings for fiscal years 2003 through 2006 and the first quarter of 2007 after an internal audit found that certain employees had changed corporate account balances to meet quarterly financial targets.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In July 2010, the SEC announced charges against several senior Dell executives, including Dell Chairman and CEO Michael Dell, former CEO Kevin Rollins, and former CFO James Schneider, "with failing to disclose material information to investors and using fraudulent accounting to make it falsely appear that the company was consistently meeting Wall Street earnings targets and reducing its operating expenses." Dell, inc. was fined $100 million, with Michael Dell personally fined $4 million.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Michael_Dell_resumes_CEO_role">Michael Dell resumes CEO role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Michael Dell resumes CEO role"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After four out of five quarterly earnings reports were below expectations, Rollins resigned as president and CEO on January 31, 2007, and founder Michael Dell assumed the role of CEO again.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-Inc-Feb-2007-8-K_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-Inc-Feb-2007-8-K-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 1, 2007, the company issued a preliminary quarterly earnings report showing gross sales of $14.4 billion, down 5% year-over-year, and net income of $687 million (30 cents per share), down 33%. Net earnings would have declined even more if not for the effects of eliminated employee bonuses, which accounted for six cents per share. NASDAQ extended the company's deadline for filing financials to May 4.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dell_2.0_and_downsizing">Dell 2.0 and downsizing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dell 2.0 and downsizing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dell announced a change campaign called "Dell 2.0," reducing the number of employees and diversifying the company's products.<sup id="cite_ref-director1_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-director1-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> While chairman of the board after relinquishing his CEO position, Michael Dell still had significant input in the company during Rollins' years as CEO. With the return of Michael Dell as CEO, the company saw changes in operations, the exodus of many senior vice-presidents and new personnel brought in from outside the company.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Michael Dell announced a number of initiatives and plans (part of the "Dell 2.0" initiative) to improve the company's financial performance. These include elimination of 2006 bonuses for employees with some discretionary awards, reduction in the number of managers reporting directly to Michael Dell from 20 to 12, and reduction of "<a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a>". Jim Schneider retired as CFO and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Carty" class="mw-redirect" title="Donald Carty">Donald Carty</a>, as the company came under an SEC probe for its accounting practices.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 23, 2008, Dell announced the closure of one of its biggest Canadian call-centers in <a href="/wiki/Kanata,_Ontario" title="Kanata, Ontario">Kanata, Ontario</a>, terminating approximately 1100 employees, with 500 of those redundancies effective on the spot, and with the official closure of the center scheduled for the summer. The call-center had opened in 2006 after the city of <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a> won a bid to host it. Less than a year later, Dell planned to double its workforce to nearly 3,000 workers add a new building. These plans were reversed, due to a high <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">Canadian dollar</a> that made the Ottawa staff relatively expensive, and also as part of Dell's turnaround, which involved moving these call-center jobs offshore to cut costs.
<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> 
The company had also announced the shutdown of its <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, office, losing 900 jobs. In total, Dell announced the ending of about 8,800 jobs in 2007–2008&#160;— 10% of its workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 2000s, Dell's "configure to order" approach of manufacturing—delivering individual PCs configured to customer specifications from its US facilities was no longer as efficient or competitive with high-volume Asian contract manufacturers as PCs became powerful low-cost commodities.<sup id="cite_ref-statesman_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statesman-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-statesman1_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statesman1-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Dell closed plants that produced desktop computers for the North American market, including the Mort Topfer Manufacturing Center in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin, Texas</a> (original location)<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon,_Tennessee" title="Lebanon, Tennessee">Lebanon, Tennessee</a> (opened in 1999) in 2008 and early 2009, respectively. The desktop production plant in <a href="/wiki/Winston-Salem,_North_Carolina" title="Winston-Salem, North Carolina">Winston-Salem, North Carolina</a>, received <a href="/wiki/US$" class="mw-redirect" title="US$">US$</a>280 million in incentives from the state and opened in 2005 (equivalent to $419,900,000&#32;in 2023), but ceased operations in November 2010. Dell's contract with the state required them to repay the incentives for failing to meet the conditions, and they sold the North Carolina plant to Herbalife.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theregister.co.uk_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theregister.co.uk-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bizjournals.com_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bizjournals.com-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Much work was transferred to manufacturers in Asia and Mexico, or some of Dell's own factories overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-statesman1_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statesman1-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> On January 8, 2009, Dell announced the closure of its manufacturing plant in Limerick, Ireland, with the loss of 1,900 jobs and the transfer of production to its plant in <a href="/wiki/%C5%81od%C5%BA" class="mw-redirect" title="Łodź">Łodź</a> in Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempts_at_diversification">Attempts at diversification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Attempts at diversification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG/220px-IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG/330px-IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG/440px-IFA_2010_Internationale_Funkausstellung_Berlin_57.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a><figcaption>Dell Streak smartphone</figcaption></figure>
<p>The release of Apple's <a href="/wiki/IPad" title="IPad">iPad</a> <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computer</a> had a negative impact on Dell and other major PC vendors, as consumers switched away from desktop and laptop PCs. Dell's own mobility division has not managed success with developing smartphones or tablets, whether running Windows or <a href="/wiki/Google_Android" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Android">Google Android</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-beta.fool_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beta.fool-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dell_Streak" title="Dell Streak">Dell Streak</a> was a failure commercially and critically due to its outdated OS, numerous bugs, and low resolution screen. <i>InfoWorld</i> suggested that Dell and other OEMs saw tablets as a short-term, low-investment opportunity running <a href="/wiki/Google_Android" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Android">Google Android</a>, an approach that neglected user interface and failed to gain long term market traction with consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Dell has responded by pushing higher-end PCs, such as the XPS line of notebooks, which do not compete with the <a href="/wiki/Apple_iPad" class="mw-redirect" title="Apple iPad">Apple iPad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kindle_Fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Kindle Fire">Kindle Fire</a> tablets.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The growing popularity of smartphones and tablet computers instead of PCs drove Dell's consumer segment to an operating loss in Q3 2012. In December 2012, Dell suffered its first decline in holiday sales in five years, despite the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">Windows 8</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the shrinking PC industry, Dell continued to lose market share, as it dropped below <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a> in 2011 to fall to number three in the world. Dell and fellow American contemporary Hewlett Packard came under pressure from Asian PC manufacturers Lenovo, <a href="/wiki/Asus" title="Asus">Asus</a>, and Acer, all of which had lower production costs and were willing to accept lower profit margins. In addition, while the Asian PC vendors had been improving their quality and design—for instance, Lenovo's <a href="/wiki/ThinkPad" title="ThinkPad">ThinkPad</a> series was winning corporate customers away from Dell's laptops—Dell's customer service and reputation had been slipping.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Dell remained the second-most profitable PC vendor, as it took 13 percent of operating profits in the PC industry during Q4 2012, behind Apple's Mac that took 45 percent, seven percent at Hewlett Packard, six percent at Lenovo and Asus, and one percent for Acer.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dell attempted to offset its declining PC business, which still accounted for half of its revenue and generates steady cash flow,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> by expanding into the enterprise market with servers, networking, software, and services.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It avoided many of the acquisition write-downs and management turnover that plagued its chief rival Hewlett Packard.<sup id="cite_ref-beta.fool_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beta.fool-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chron.com_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron.com-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Despite spending $13 billion on acquisitions to diversify its portfolio beyond hardware,<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> the company was unable to convince the market that it could thrive or made the transformation in the post-PC world,<sup id="cite_ref-chron.com_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron.com-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> as it suffered continued declines in revenue and share price.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economist_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Dell's market share in the corporate segment was previously a "moat" against rivals but this has no longer been the case as sales and profits have fallen precipitously.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2013_buyout">2013 buyout</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2013 buyout"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After several weeks of rumors, which started around January 11, 2013, Dell announced on February 5, 2013, that it had struck a $24.4 billion (equivalent to $31,470,000,000&#32;in 2023) <a href="/wiki/Leveraged_buyout" title="Leveraged buyout">leveraged buyout</a> deal, that would have delisted its shares from the NASDAQ and Hong Kong Stock Exchange and taken it private.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reuters" title="Reuters">Reuters</a> reported that Michael Dell and <a href="/wiki/Silver_Lake_Partners" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver Lake Partners">Silver Lake Partners</a>, aided by a $2 billion loan from <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, would acquire the public shares at $13.65 apiece.<sup id="cite_ref-buyout_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buyout-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The $24.4 billion buyout was projected to be the largest leveraged buyout backed by private equity since the <a href="/wiki/2007_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 financial crisis">2007 financial crisis</a> (equivalent to $34,550,000,000&#32;in 2023).<sup id="cite_ref-buyout2_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buyout2-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> It is also the largest technology buyout ever, surpassing the 2006 buyout of <a href="/wiki/Freescale_Semiconductor" title="Freescale Semiconductor">Freescale Semiconductor</a> for $17.5 billion (equivalent to $25,450,000,000&#32;in 2023).<sup id="cite_ref-buyout2_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buyout2-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Michael Dell said of the February offer "I believe this transaction will open an exciting new chapter for Dell, our customers and team members".<sup id="cite_ref-buyout3_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buyout3-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Dell rival Lenovo responded to the buyout, saying, "the financial actions of some of our traditional competitors will not substantially change our outlook."<sup id="cite_ref-buyout3_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buyout3-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Blackstone_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackstone Group">Blackstone Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Icahn" title="Carl Icahn">Carl Icahn</a> expressed interest in purchasing Dell.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In April 2013, Blackstone withdrew their offer, citing deteriorating business.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Other private equity firms such as KKR &amp; Co. and TPG Capital declined to submit alternative bids for Dell, citing the uncertain market for personal computers and competitive pressures, so the "wide-open bidding war" never materialized.<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Analysts said that the biggest challenge facing Silver Lake would be to find an "exit strategy" to profit from its investment, which would be when the company would hold an IPO to go public again, and one warned "But even if you can get a $25bn enterprise value for Dell, it will take years to get out."<sup id="cite_ref-Gelles_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gelles-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2013, Michael Dell joined his board in voting for the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The following August he reached a deal with the special committee on the board for $13.88 per share, a raised price of $13.75 plus a special dividend of 13 cents, as well as a change to the voting rules.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The $13.88 cash offer (plus a $.08 per share dividend for the third fiscal quarter) was accepted on September 12<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and closed on October 30, 2013, ending Dell's 25-year run as a publicly-traded company.
</p><p>After the buyout, the newly private Dell offered a Voluntary Separation Program that they expected to reduce their workforce by up to seven percent. The reception to the program so exceeded the expectations that Dell may be forced to hire new staff to make up for the losses.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recent history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 19, 2015, Dell, alongside <a href="/wiki/Arm_Holdings" title="Arm Holdings">Arm Holdings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cisco" title="Cisco">Cisco Systems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/OpenFog_Consortium" title="OpenFog Consortium">OpenFog Consortium</a>, to promote interests and development in <a href="/wiki/Fog_computing" title="Fog computing">fog computing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_of_EMC">Acquisition of EMC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Acquisition of EMC"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On October 12, 2015, <a href="/wiki/Dell_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Dell Inc.">Dell Inc.</a> announced its intent to acquire <a href="/wiki/EMC_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="EMC Corporation">EMC Corporation</a> in a cash-and-stock deal valued at $67 billion (equivalent to $84,210,000,000&#32;in 2023), which has been considered the largest-ever acquisition in the technology sector.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc-dellemc_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-dellemc-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> As part of the acquisition, Dell would take over EMC's 81% stake in the cloud-computing and virtualization company <a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-EMC_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-EMC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> This would combine Dell's enterprise server, personal computer, and mobile businesses with EMC's enterprise storage business in a significant Vertical merger of IT giants. Dell would pay $24.05 per share of EMC, and $9.05 per share of <a href="/wiki/Tracking_stock" title="Tracking stock">tracking stock</a> in <a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj-emcdell_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-emcdell-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt-emcdell_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-emcdell-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc-dellemc_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-dellemc-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The announcement came two years after Dell Inc. returned to private ownership, claiming that it faced bleak prospects and would need several years out of the public eye to rebuild its business.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-20130329_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-20130329-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> It was thought that the company's value had roughly doubled since then.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-2015-10-12_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-2015-10-12-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> EMC was being pressured by <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Management_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Elliott Management Corporation">Elliott Management</a>, a hedge fund holding 2.2% of EMC's stock, to reorganize their unusual "Federation" structure, in which EMC's divisions were effectively being run as independent companies. Elliott argued<sup id="cite_ref-BusinessWire-2014-10-08_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWire-2014-10-08-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> this structure deeply undervalued EMC's core "EMC II" data storage business, and that increasing competition between EMC II and VMware products was confusing the market and hindering both companies. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> estimated that in 2014 Dell had revenue of $27.3 billion (equivalent to $34,610,000,000&#32;in 2023) from personal computers and $8.9 billion from servers, while EMC had $16.5 billion from EMC II, $1 billion from <a href="/wiki/RSA_Security" title="RSA Security">RSA Security</a>, $6 billion from <a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a>, and $230 million from <a href="/wiki/Pivotal_Software" title="Pivotal Software">Pivotal Software</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-2015-10-13_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-2015-10-13-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> EMC owns around 80 percent of the stock of VMware.<sup id="cite_ref-FT-Lex-2015-10-12_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT-Lex-2015-10-12-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The proposed acquisition will maintain VMware as a separate company, held via a new <a href="/wiki/Tracking_stock" title="Tracking stock">tracking stock</a>, while the other parts of EMC will be rolled into Dell.<sup id="cite_ref-FT-2015-10-12_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT-2015-10-12-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Once the acquisition closes Dell will again publish quarterly financial results, having ceased these on going private in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The combined business was expected to address the markets for <a href="/wiki/Converged_storage#Scale-out_architecture" title="Converged storage">scale-out architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Converged_infrastructure" title="Converged infrastructure">converged infrastructure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing#Private_cloud" title="Cloud computing">private cloud computing</a>, playing to the strengths of both EMC and Dell.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ-2015-10-13_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-2015-10-13-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Economist-2015-10-12_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-2015-10-12-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Commentators have questioned the deal, with <a href="/wiki/FBR_Capital_Markets" title="FBR Capital Markets">FBR Capital Markets</a> saying that though it makes a "ton of sense" for Dell, it's a "nightmare scenario that would lack strategic synergies" for EMC.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters-2015-10-08_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-2015-10-08-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <i>Fortune</i> said there was a lot for Dell to like in EMC's portfolio, but "does it all add up enough to justify tens of billions of dollars for the entire package? Probably not."<sup id="cite_ref-Fortune-2015-10-08_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune-2015-10-08-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Register" title="The Register">The Register</a></i> reported the view of <a href="/wiki/William_Blair_%26_Company" title="William Blair &amp; Company">William Blair &amp; Company</a> that the merger would "blow up the current IT chess board", forcing other IT infrastructure vendors to restructure to achieve scale and vertical integration.<sup id="cite_ref-Register-2015-10-09_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Register-2015-10-09-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The value of VMware stock fell 10% after the announcement, valuing the deal at around $63–64bn rather than the $67bn originally reported.<sup id="cite_ref-FT-Lex-Live-2015-10-13_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT-Lex-Live-2015-10-13-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Key investors backing the deal besides Dell were Singapore's <a href="/wiki/Temasek_Holdings" title="Temasek Holdings">Temasek Holdings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silver_Lake_Partners" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver Lake Partners">Silver Lake Partners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 7, 2016, Dell completed the merger with EMC, which involved the issuance of $45.9 billion (equivalent to $57,140,000,000&#32;in 2023) in debt and $4.4 billion (equivalent to $5,478,000,000&#32;in 2023) of common stock.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> At the time, some analysts claimed that Dell's acquisition of the former Iomega could harm the <a href="/wiki/LenovoEMC" class="mw-redirect" title="LenovoEMC">LenovoEMC</a> partnership.<sup id="cite_ref-IomegaPart.TheRegister_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IomegaPart.TheRegister-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, Dell announced intentions to become a publicly traded company again by paying $21.7 billion (equivalent to $25,940,000,000&#32;in 2023) in both cash and stock to buy back shares from its stake in VMware, offering shareholders roughly 60 cents on the dollar as part of the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dell-EMC_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-EMC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In November, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Icahn" title="Carl Icahn">Carl Icahn</a> (9.3% owner of Dell) sued the company over plans to go public.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> As a result of pressure from Icahn and other <a href="/wiki/Activist_shareholder" class="mw-redirect" title="Activist shareholder">activist investors</a>, Dell renegotiated the deal, ultimately offering shareholders about 80% of market value. As part of this deal, Dell once again became a public company, with the original Dell computer business and Dell EMC operating under the newly created parent, <b><a href="/wiki/Dell_Technologies" title="Dell Technologies">Dell Technologies</a></b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-EMC_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-EMC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Post-acquisition, Dell was re-organized with a new parent company, Dell Technologies; Dell's consumer and workstation businesses are internally referred to as the Dell Client Solutions Group, and is one of the company's three main business divisions alongside <a href="/wiki/Dell_EMC" title="Dell EMC">Dell EMC</a> and <a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsjemcdell_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsjemcdell-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2021 (equivalent to $14,420,000,000&#32;in 2023), Dell reported $94 billion (equivalent to $104,280,000,000&#32;in 2023) in sales and $13 billion operating cash flow during 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Dell-EMC_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-EMC-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 1, 2024, Dell's stock hit all time high after earnings. It delivered a strong performance from its artificial intelligence unit that sent shares up nearly 40%, its highest daily gain since the company went public in 2018. <sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dell_and_AMD">Dell and AMD</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dell and AMD"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When Dell acquired <a href="/wiki/Alienware" title="Alienware">Alienware</a> early in 2006, some Alienware systems had <a href="/wiki/AMD" title="AMD">AMD</a> chips. On August 17, 2006, a Dell press release<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> stated that starting in September, Dell Dimension desktop computers would have AMD processors and that later in the year Dell would release a two-socket, quad-processor server using AMD <a href="/wiki/Opteron" title="Opteron">Opteron</a> chips, moving away from Dell's tradition of only offering <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> processors in Dell PCs.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/CNET" title="CNET">CNET</a>'s News.com on August 17, 2006, cited Dell's CEO Kevin Rollins as attributing the move to AMD processors to lower costs and to AMD technology.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> AMD's senior VP in commercial business, Marty Seyer, stated: "Dell's wider embrace of AMD processor-based offerings is a win for Dell, for the industry and most importantly for Dell customers."
</p><p>On October 23, 2006, Dell announced new AMD-based servers&#160;&#8212; the PowerEdge 6950 and the <a href="/wiki/PowerEdge" title="PowerEdge">PowerEdge</a> SC1435.
</p><p>On November 1, 2006, Dell's website began offering notebooks based on AMD processors (the Inspiron 1501 with a 15.4-inch (390&#160;mm) display) with the choice of a single-core MK-36 processor, dual-core Turion X2 chips or Mobile Sempron.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, Dell released the AlienWare 17 gaming laptop. The model was primarily based on NVIDIA GeForce GTX 1080 systems.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dell_and_desktop_Linux">Dell and desktop Linux</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dell and desktop Linux"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1998, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a> asked Dell (and five other major <a href="/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" title="Original equipment manufacturer">OEMs</a>) to offer alternate operating systems to <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, specifically including <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, for which "there is clearly a growing interest".<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Possibly coincidentally, Dell started offering Linux notebook systems that "cost no more than their Windows 98 counterparts" in 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and soon expanded, with Dell becoming "the first major manufacturer to offer Linux across its full product line".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> However, by early 2001 Dell had "disbanded its Linux business unit."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 26, 2007, Dell announced that it had commenced a program to sell and distribute a range of computers with pre-installed <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> distributions as an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>. Dell indicated that <a href="/wiki/Novell" title="Novell">Novell</a>'s <a href="/wiki/SUSE_Linux" title="SUSE Linux">SUSE</a> Linux would appear first.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> However, the next day, Dell announced that its previous announcement related to certifying the hardware as ready to work with Novell SUSE Linux and that it (Dell) had no plans to sell systems pre-installed with Linux in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> On March 28, 2007, Dell announced that it would begin shipping some desktops and laptops with Linux pre-installed, although it did not specify which distribution of Linux or which hardware would lead.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> On April 18, a report appeared suggesting that Michael Dell used <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu" title="Ubuntu">Ubuntu</a> on one of his home systems.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> On May 1, 2007, Dell announced it would ship the Ubuntu Linux distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On May 24, 2007, Dell started selling models with Ubuntu Linux 7.04 pre-installed: a laptop, a budget computer, and a high-end PC.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 27, 2007, Dell announced on its Direct2Dell blog that it planned to offer more pre-loaded systems (the new <a href="/wiki/Dell_Inspiron" title="Dell Inspiron">Dell Inspiron</a> desktops and laptops). After the <a href="/wiki/Dell_IdeaStorm" title="Dell IdeaStorm">IdeaStorm</a> site supported extending the bundles beyond the US market, Dell later announced more international marketing.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> On August 7, 2007, Dell officially announced that it would offer one notebook and one desktop in the UK, France and Germany with Ubuntu "pre-installed". At <a href="/wiki/LinuxWorld_Conference_and_Expo" title="LinuxWorld Conference and Expo">LinuxWorld</a> 2007 Dell announced plans to provide <a href="/wiki/Novell" title="Novell">Novell</a>'s <a href="/wiki/SUSE_Linux_Enterprise_Desktop" class="mw-redirect" title="SUSE Linux Enterprise Desktop">SUSE Linux Enterprise Desktop</a> on selected models in China, "factory-installed".<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> On November 30, 2007, Dell reported shipping 40,000 Ubuntu PCs.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> On January 24, 2008, Dell in Germany, Spain, France, and the United Kingdom launched a second laptop, an XPS M1330 with <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu" title="Ubuntu">Ubuntu</a> 7.10, for 849 euro or GBP 599 upwards.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> On February 18, 2008, Dell announced that the <a href="/wiki/Dell_Inspiron_1525" title="Dell Inspiron 1525">Inspiron 1525</a> would have Ubuntu as an optional operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> On February 22, 2008, Dell announced plans to sell Ubuntu in Canada and in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> From September 16, 2008, Dell has shipped both <a href="/wiki/Dell_Ubuntu_Netbook_Remix" class="mw-redirect" title="Dell Ubuntu Netbook Remix">Dell Ubuntu Netbook Remix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a> Home versions of the <a href="/wiki/Dell_Inspiron_Mini_9" class="mw-redirect" title="Dell Inspiron Mini 9">Inspiron Mini 9</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dell_Inspiron_Mini_12" class="mw-redirect" title="Dell Inspiron Mini 12">Inspiron Mini 12</a>. As of November 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> Dell shipped the Inspiron Mini laptops with Ubuntu version 8.04.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2021, Dell continues to offer select laptops and workstations with Ubuntu Linux pre-installed, under the "Developer Edition" moniker.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_Dell_marketing_slogans">List of Dell marketing slogans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: List of Dell marketing slogans"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
